Khilafah (Representation): Definition, Condition, and Application
Explaining khilafah, its condition, and application to oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khilafah, state its condition (the original must be potentially existent), and apply it to distinguish between valid and invalid representation in oaths and purification.

Core Idea
Representation (khilafah) requires the original to be potentially existent; impossible originals cannot have substitutes.

In Hanafi usul, the concept of khilafah governs when one thing can stand in for another in legal rulings. This lesson unpacks its definition, its foundational proof from the Quran, and a critical condition: the original must be potentially existent. Through the intra-Hanafi dispute on tayammum and the rulings on false oaths, you will see how this condition operates and why it matters for deriving correct fiqh positions.

Guided Path
1
Define khilafah and identify its textual basis
Start by noting that khilafah is a substitute ruling that takes the place of the original when the original is absent. The primary textual evidence is Quran 5:6, which makes pure dust a substitute for water in purification when water is unavailable.
The Hanafi principle: khilafah is established only by nass (explicit text), never by qiyas or ra'y.
2
Pinpoint the essential condition for valid khilafah
The passage states: the original must be absent but potentially existent (muhtamal al-wujud). If the original cannot possibly exist by nature, no representation can be established.
This condition prevents representation from being attached to inherently impossible acts.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on tayammum
Contrast the view of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf (khilafah between water and dust) with that of Muhammad and Zufar (khilafah between wudu and tayammum). This leads to a furuʿ: whether a mutayammim can lead those with wudu in prayer.
The difference hinges on whether the representation is between the mediums or between the acts of purification.
4
Apply the condition to problematic oaths
Examine yamin ghamus (false oath about the past) and an oath to touch the sky. In the first, the original (fulfillment) is impossible because the past cannot be changed, so no expiation is due. In the second, touch is possible by miracle, but present inability triggers expiation.
The condition of potential existence directly determines the legal outcome.

Source Text

Possible by breaking the norm, but inability is apparent in the present, so expiation is obligatory for him. Translation: And according to al-Shafi'i it is necessary, meaning tayammum... etc. Then the author corrected his statement 'this ruling with us is absolute' and said 'but the representation is between water and dust'... This representation is between water and dust according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf because Allah said 'if you do not find water then perform tayammum with pure dust'. So Allah made dust a substitute for water. According to Muhammad and Zufar, it is between wudu and tayammum because both are obtained from water and dust; not between the two mediums. Then it is built upon this difference the issue of a mutayammim leading those with wudu. According to the two shaykhs it is permissible because dust is a substitute for water, but tayammum is not a substitute for wudu; they are equal. So one can follow the other. According to Muhammad and Zufar it is not permissible because tayammum is a substitute for wudu, so the weaker cannot lead. And representation is only established by text, not by analogy, just as the original ruling is not established by opinion and analogy. Its condition is that the original is absent but potentially existent, so that the ruling can be transferred to the substitute. If the original cannot possibly exist, then representation is invalid. This condition appears in the case of a false oath (yamin ghamus) and the oath of touching the sky, because in a false oath expiation is not obligatory because fulfillment is impossible. Similarly, fulfilling an oath to touch the sky is impossible except by miracle, but since inability is apparent, expiation is obligatory.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the concept of khilafah (representation) and its conditions, expanding on earlier discussions. It begins with the possibility of breaking a norm (khāriq al-ʿādah) for prophets and awliya but states that in the present inability, expiation becomes obligatory. The core topic is the khilafah between water and dust in tayammum as established by the Quranic verse (5:6). The author contrasts the positions of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, who see khilafah between water and dust, with Muhammad and Zufar, who see it between wudu and tayammum. This difference leads to a furuʿ: the permissibility of a mutayammim leading those with wudu. According to the two shaykhs, it is permissible because tayammum is not a substitute for wudu but equal; according to the two imams, it is not permissible because tayammum is weaker. The passage then states a general usul rule: representation is only proven by nass (text), not by ra'y or qiyas. Its condition is that the original must be potentially existent (muḥtamal al-wujūd). This condition is illustrated with yamin ghamus (false oath about the past) and an oath to touch the sky—cases where fulfillment is impossible, so expiation is not obligatory because representation cannot apply.

Key Terms
الخلافة (al-khilāfah): Representation; one thing standing in for another in legal rulings, established only by nass.
يمين غموس (yamīn ghamūs): A false oath about a past event, where fulfillment is impossible because the past cannot be changed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a valid khilafah (representation)?
A) The original must be completely impossible.
B) The original must be absent but potentially existent.
C) The substitute must be identical to the original.
D) There is no condition.

Key Takeaway
Khilafah requires the original to be potentially existent; impossible actions like changing the past cannot have substitutes, so no expiation is due for false oaths about the past.

Definition of Sabab Haqiqi and Its Distinction from 'Alamah
Defining sabab haqiqi and differentiating it from 'alamah and other types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sabab haqiqi (true cause) and distinguish it from 'alamah, 'illah, and shart, applying the definition to determine liability in causal chains.

Core Idea
A true cause leads to a ruling without exerting effective influence; it is a mere path, not a creator.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the categories of sabab (cause) is crucial for correctly attributing rulings and liabilities. This lesson defines sabab haqiqi (true cause) and clarifies its distinction from other related terms like 'alamah (sign), 'illah (effective cause), and shart (condition). We will explore a concrete example of guidance to theft to see how the definition works, and then examine a fatwa exception where later jurists modified the ruling due to societal corruption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its category
The passage defines sabab haqiqi as the first division of the four categories of sabab. Note that sabab itself is one of the four things attached to rulings (sabab, shart, mani', 'alamah?).
2
Parse the definition: path vs. sign
The definition states sabab haqiqi is a path (tariq) leading to the ruling, unlike 'alamah which only indicates. Also, it is not like 'illah which directly obligates or brings about the ruling, nor like shart which is a prerequisite for existence.
Key distinction: sabab haqiqi leads but does NOT have ta'thir (effective influence).
3
Understand the negative conditions
The sabab haqiqi has no effect on the obligation (wujub) or existence (wujud) of the ruling. It is merely a prerequisite in the chain, but its presence does not force the ruling; the free choice of the agent intervenes.
If it had direct effect, it would be a cause resembling 'illah (shabih al-illah).
4
Analyze the example: guidance to theft
A person guiding another to steal his property is a true cause (sabab haqiqi) because it leads to the theft but does not cause it directly. The thief's free will and choice are the intervening 'illah. Therefore, the guide is not liable (daman) for the theft.
The guide is sahib sabab mahd (mere possessor of a cause), not sahib 'illah.
5
Apply to the slanderer case and note the fatwa exception
Analogously, one who slanders to a tyrant causing loss should not be liable according to the principle. However, later Hanafi jurists gave a fatwa of liability due to the corruption of the times and prevalence of slander (sa'y). This shows that context can supersede the original ruling in fatwa.
This highlights the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh: principles are applied with consideration of public interest (maslahah) and changing circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْقِسمُ الثَّانِى مِنَ التَقْسِيمِ الْمَذْكُورِ فِى أوَّلِ الْفَصْلِ وَهُوَ مَا يَتَعَلَّق به الأَحْكَامُ فَأَرْبَعَةُ الأَوَّلُ السَّبَبَ وَهُوَ أَقْسَامُ أَرْبَعةُ الأَوَّلُ سَبَبٌ حَقِيقِيُّ وَهُوَ مَا يَكُونَ طَرِيقًا إلى الحكم أى مُفْضِيًا إِلَيْهِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْعَلامَةِ فَإِنَّهَا دَالَّةٌ عَلَى الْحُكْمِ أَيْ عَلَيْهِ لَأَنَّهَا مُقْتَضِيَةٌ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يُضافَ إِلَيْهِ وُجُوْبُ الْحُكْمِ كَمَا يُضاف ذَالِكَ إِلَى الْعِلَّةِ وَلَا الْوُجُودَ كَمَا يُضاف ذَالِكَ إِلَى الشَّرْطِ وَلَا يُعْقَلُ فِيْهِ مَعَانِي الْعِلَلِ بِوَجْهٍ مِنَ الْوُجُوهِ بِحَيْثُ لَا يَكُونُ لَهُ تَاثِيرٌ فِي وُجُوبِ الْحُكْمِ أَصْلاً لَا بِوَاسِطَةٍ وَلَا بِغَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ إِذَا كَانَ كَذَالِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ سَبَبًا حَقِيقِيًّا بَلْ سَبَبًا لَهُ شِبْهَةُ الْعِلَّةِ أو سَبَبًا فِيهِ مَعْنَى الْعِلَّةِ لِكِنْ يَتَخَلَّلُ بَيْنَهُ أَى بَيْنَ السَّبَبِ وَبَيْنَ الْحُكم علةٌ لاتُضافُ إِلَى السَّبَبِ إِذَا كَانَتْ مُضَافَةً إِلَى السَّبَبِ وَالْحُكْمِ مُضَافٌ إِلَيْهَا لَكَانَ السَّبَبُ عِلَّةَ الْعِلَّةِ لَا سَبَبًا حَقِيقِيًّا عَلَى مَا سَيَأْتِي كَدَلَالَةِ إِنْسَانٍ عَلَى مَالِ إِنْسَانٍ أَوْ نَفْسِهِ لِيَسْرِقَهُ أَوْ لِيَقْتُلَهُ فَإِنَّهَا سَبَبٌ حَقِيقِيٌّ لِلسَّرَقَةِ وَالْقَتْلِ لِأَنَّهَا تُقْضِي إِلَيْهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُوجِبَةً أَوْ مُوجِدَةً لَهُ وَلَا تَاثِيرَ لَهَا فِي فِعْلِ السَّرَقَةِ أَصْلاً لِكِنْ تَخَلَّلَ بَيْنَ الدَّلَالَةِ وَبَيْنَ السَّرَقَةِ عِلَّةٌ غَيْرُ مُضَافَةٍ إِلَى الدَّلَالَةِ وَهُوَ فِعْلُ السَّارِقِ الْمُخْتَارِ وَقَصْدُهُ إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ أَنَّ مَنْ دَلَّ أَحَدًا عَلَى فِعْلِ سَوْءٍ يَفْعَلُهُ الْمَدْلُولُ الْبَتَّةَ بَلْ عَسَى اللهُ يُوَفِّقُهُ عَلَى تَرْكِهِ مَعَ دَلَالَتِهِ فَإِنْ وَقَعَ مِنْهُ السَّرَقَةُ أو الْقَتْلُ لَا يَضْمَنُ الدَّالُّ شَيْئًا لِأَنَّهُ صَاحِبُ سَبَبٍ مَحْضٍ لَا صَاحِبُ عِلَّةٍ وَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا يُضَمَّنَ مَنْ سَعَى إِلَى سُلْطَانٍ ظَالِمٍ فِى حَقٍ أَحَدٍ بِغَيْرِ حَقٍ حَتَّى غَرِمَهُ مَالاً لِأَنَّهُ صَاحِبُ سَبَبٍ مَحْضٍ لِكِنْ أَفْتَى الْمُتَاخِرُونَ بِضَمَانِهِ لِفَسَادِ الزَّمَانِ بِالسَّعْيِ الْبَاطِلِ وَكَثْرَةِ السُّعَاةِ فِيهِ.
As for the second part of the division mentioned at the beginning of the chapter, it is that which is attached to the rulings, so it is four. The first is the cause (sabab), and it has four divisions. The first is the true cause, which is that which is a path to the ruling, i.e., leading to it in general, unlike the sign ('alamah), for it is indicative of the ruling, i.e., upon it, because it is a requirement [of the ruling] without the obligation of the ruling being attributed to it as it is attributed to the illah, nor existence as it is attributed to the condition, and the meanings of the illal are not understood in it in any way, such that it has no effect on the obligation of the ruling at all, neither directly nor indirectly. If it were so, it would not be a true cause but a cause that resembles the illah or a cause containing the meaning of the illah, but an illah intervenes between it and the ruling that is not attributed to the cause. If it were attributed to the cause and the ruling were attributed to it, then the cause would be the cause of the illah, not a true cause, as will come. An example is a person leading another to his property or himself to steal it or kill him, for that is a true cause for the theft and murder because it leads to it without being causative or existential for it, and it has no effect on the act of theft at all. But between the indication and the theft intervenes an illah not attributed to the indication, which is the free act of the thief and his intention, for it is not necessary that whoever leads someone to a bad act, the led person does it; perhaps Allah grants him success to abandon it despite his indication. If theft or murder occurs, the one who indicated does not guarantee anything because he is a mere possessor of a cause, not a possessor of an illah. On this basis, it should be that one who slanders to a tyrannical ruler against someone unjustly until he causes him loss of property should not be held liable, because he is a mere possessor of a cause, but the later jurists gave a fatwa of liability due to the corruption of the time and the prevalence of false slander.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a definition of a key usul term: sabab haqiqi (true cause). It is the second part of the division of things that are attached to legal rulings. The author first divides this into four categories (sabab, shart, mani', 'alamah?), then focuses on sabab, which itself has four types, the first being sabab haqiqi. The definition: sabab haqiqi is something that is a path (tariq) to the ruling, i.e., it leads to the ruling in general (fi al-jumlah), but unlike 'alamah (sign), which merely indicates the ruling without leading to it. Also, unlike 'illah, which directly causes the obligation or existence of the ruling, the sabab haqiqi does not have any effective influence (ta'thir) on the obligation of the ruling, directly or indirectly. If it did have such influence, it would not be a true cause but would be a cause that resembles 'illah or contains its meaning. The author then gives an example: a person guiding someone to his own property or person to steal or kill. This guidance is a true cause for the theft or murder because it leads to it, but it does not necessitate it; the thief's free choice and act intervenes. Therefore, if the theft occurs, the guide is not liable because he is a mere possessor of a cause (sahib sabab mahd), not a possessor of an illah. The author then applies this to a slanderer (sa'i) who goes to a tyrant against someone unjustly; according to the principle, he should not be liable, but later jurists have given fatwas of liability due to the corruption of the times and the prevalence of false slander. This shows the importance of context in fatwa.

Key Terms
سبَب حقيقي (sabab ḥaqīqī): A true cause: something that is a path to a ruling, leading to it without having any effective influence on its obligation or existence.
علامة (ʿalāmah): A sign: an indicator of the ruling without being a path to it (i.e., it does not lead to the ruling).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the one who guides a thief to steal not liable for the theft according to the definition of sabab haqiqi?
A) Because guidance is not a cause at all.
B) Because guidance is a sabab haqiqi that leads to theft but does not have effective influence; the thief's free choice intervenes.
C) Because the guide intended only good.
D) Because theft is not prohibited.

Key Takeaway
A true cause (sabab haqiqi) leads to a ruling without causing it, so the agent is not liable; liability requires direct effective cause ('illah).

Yamin Ghamus and Oath to Touch the Sky
Explaining rulings on false past oaths and impossible oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between yamin ghamus and an impossible oath, and state the Hanafi ruling for each regarding kaffarah.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is a substitute for fulfilling an oath; if fulfilling is impossible, no kaffarah is due.

This passage explains two special cases of oaths: a false oath about the past (yamin ghamus) and an oath to perform an impossible act like touching the sky. The Hanafi school derives rulings based on the ability to fulfill the oath. Understanding the reasoning behind these rulings will deepen your grasp of how legal obligations adapt to human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oaths
Read the passage and list the two categories of oaths discussed: yamin ghamus (false oath about the past) and an oath to touch the sky (an impossible act).
The second oath is an example of an oath that is currently impossible for the ordinary person.
2
Determine the ruling on kaffarah
For each type, state whether kaffarah is wajib or not according to the Hanafi school. The passage gives the answer: no kaffarah for yamin ghamus, and kaffarah is wajib for the oath to touch the sky.
3
Analyze the underlying reasoning
Identify the talil (legal reasoning): kaffarah is a substitute (khalifah) for fulfillment. If fulfillment is impossible, there is no need for a substitute. For yamin ghamus, the past cannot be changed; for the impossible oath, the ordinary person is currently unable, so kaffarah becomes wajib.
The exception: prophets and awliya can touch the sky by miracle, so for them the oath is possible and the ruling differs.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Consider an oath to fly unaided. Apply the same talil: the person is currently unable, so kaffarah is wajib. If the person later acquires ability, the ruling may change.
This illustrates the taxmim (generalization) of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغَمُوسِ وَالْخَلْفِ مَس السَّمَاءِ يَمِيْن غموس واقعہ ماضیہ پر جھوٹی قسم کھانا ) اور آسمان چھونے کی قسم کے مشکلوں میں کیونکہ یمین غموس میں کفارہ واجب نہیں ہوتا ہے کیونکہ (کفارہ تو خلیفہ ہے ) اور یہاں قسم کا پورا کرنا ممکن ہی نہیں ہے جو کہ یمین کا اصل حکم ہے کیونکہ زمانہ ماضی تو پہلے گزر چکا ہے واپس لانے کی اس میں قدرت و طاقت نہیں ہے اسی طرح آسمان چھونے کی قسم کے سلسلے میں قسم کا پورا کرنا اور آسمان کو چھو نا نا ممکن ہے کیونکہ انبیاء اور ملا بلکہ آسمان کو چھو لیتے ہیں نیز اولیاء اللہ کے لیئے بھی بطور خرق عادت کے ممکن ہے البتہ فی الحال بظاہر عاجز ہونا یعنی وہ قسم کو پوری نہیں کر سکتا ہے لہذا اس پر کفارہ واجب ہے۔
An oath of ghamus (false swearing about a past event) and an oath of touching the sky: a false oath about a past event is swearing falsely about the past. And an oath to touch the sky is among the [impossible oaths] because for yamin ghamus, kaffarah (expiation) is not obligatory, because (kaffarah is a substitute) and here fulfilling the oath is not possible, which is the original ruling of an oath, because the past has already passed and there is no power or ability to bring it back. Similarly, in the case of an oath to touch the sky, fulfilling the oath and touching the sky is impossible, because prophets and angels actually touch the sky; also for the awliya of Allah, it is possible as a miracle. However, in the present, apparently he is unable, i.e., he cannot fulfill the oath, so kaffarah is wajib upon him.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on two types of oaths: yamin ghamus (false oath about the past) and an oath to touch the sky (an impossible act). For yamin ghamus, the author explains that no kaffarah is required because kaffarah is a substitute for fulfilling the oath, but here fulfilling the oath is impossible since the past cannot be changed. For the oath to touch the sky, although it may be possible for prophets and saints by miracle, for an ordinary person it is currently impossible, so kaffarah becomes wajib. The reasoning is that kaffarah is only due when the oath cannot be fulfilled due to inability.

Key Terms
یمین غموس (yamin ghamus): A false oath about a past event.
کفارہ (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath, typically fasting or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, is kaffarah (expiation) required for a false oath about the past (yamin ghamus)?
A) Yes, always.
B) No.
C) Yes, if the oath was deliberate.
D) No, but a different penalty applies.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is only due when the oath cannot be fulfilled; for past false oaths, fulfillment is impossible, so no kaffarah.

Three Categories of 'Illah in Name, Meaning, and Ruling
Classifying causes by name, meaning, and ruling with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given cause in Hanafi usul as an 'illah in name, meaning, or ruling, and provide examples for each type.

Core Idea
A cause in Hanafi usul may be effective immediately, delayed, or merely nominal—each with distinct legal consequences.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Anwar, the commentator expands on the classification of 'illah (cause) by considering three dimensions: whether it is a cause in name (asman), in meaning (ma'nan), and in ruling (hukman). These dimensions help distinguish between different types of causes that may be fully operative, partially operative, or only nominally so. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying causes in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three dimensions
In Hanafi usul, a cause can be evaluated in three respects: name (whether it is called a 'cause'), meaning (whether it has a real effect, ta'thir), and ruling (whether the legal consequence occurs immediately). Master these three concepts.
2
Identify the first type: cause in name, meaning, and ruling
This is the complete cause. Example: an absolute sale free from option. It is a cause in name (set for ownership), in meaning (it effects ownership), and in ruling (ownership transfers instantly).
3
Identify the second type: cause in name only
This is a suspended cause, e.g., conditional divorce ('You are divorced if you enter the house'). The utterance is called a cause but has no effect until the condition is met. It is not a cause in meaning or ruling.
4
Identify the third type: cause in name and meaning only
This cause has a real effect but the ruling is delayed. Examples: sale with option, suspended sale, time-based pronouncement, zakat nisab before the year. They are causes in name and meaning but the legal consequence is postponed.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Given a scenario (e.g., a will, a debt, a contract), determine its type by checking each dimension: Is it called a cause? Does it have an effect (ta'thir)? Does the ruling occur immediately?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا عَرَفْتَ هَذَا فَالآنَ نَشْرَعُ عَلَى مَا قَسَمَهُ الْمُصَنِّفُ فَنَقُولُ: الْأَوَّلُ: عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا وَمَعْنًى وَحُكْمًا كَالْبَيْعِ الْمُطْلَقِ لِلْمِلْكِ الْخَالِي عَنْ خِيَارِ الشَّرْطِ، فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لِأَنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْمِلْكِ وَالْمِلْكُ مُضَافٌ إِلَيْهِ، وَمَعْنًى لِأَنَّهُ يُؤَثِّرُ فِيهِ وَهُوَ مَشْرُوعٌ لِأَجْلِهِ، وَحُكْمًا لِأَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ عِنْدَ وُجُودِهِ بِلَا تَأَخُّرٍ. وَالثَّانِي: عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لَا حُكْمًا وَلَا مَعْنَى كَالْإِيجَابِ الْمُعَلَّقِ بِالشَّرْطِ وَهُوَ الَّذِي أَدْخَلَهُ فِيمَا سَبَقَ فِي السَّبَبِ الْمَجَازِيِّ، مِثْلُ قَوْلِهِ: "أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ دَخَلْتِ الدَّارَ" فَإِنَّ قَوْلَهُ "أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ" عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لِوُقُوعِ الطَّلَاقِ فَإِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لَهُ فِي الشَّرْعِ وَيُضَافُ الْحُكْمُ إِلَيْهِ عِنْدَ وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ، وَلَيْسَ عِلَّةً حُكْمًا لِأَنَّ حُكْمَهُ يَتَأَخَّرُ إِلَى وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ، وَلَا مَعْنًى إِذْ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهُ فِيهِ قَبْلَ وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ. وَمِنْ هَذَا الْقَبِيلِ الْيَمِينُ بِاللَّهِ تَعَالَى لِلْكَفَّارَةِ عَلَى مَا قَالُوا. وَالثَّالِثُ: عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا وَمَعْنًى لَا حُكْمًا كَالْبَيْعِ بِشَرْطِ الْخِيَارِ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لِلْمِلْكِ اسْمًا وَمَعْنًى، لَا حُكْمًا لِتَأَخُّرِ الْمِلْكِ إِلَى إِسْقَاطِ الْخِيَارِ. وَالْبَيْعُ الْمَوْقُوفُ عَطْفٌ عَلَيْهِ وَهُوَ مِثَالٌ ثَانٍ، وَالْإِيجَابُ الْمُضَافُ إِلَى وَقْتٍ مِثَالٌ ثَالِثٌ كَقَوْلِهِ "أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ غَدًا"، وَنِصَابُ الزَّكَاةِ قَبْلَ الْحَوْلِ مِثَالٌ رَابِعٌ. [Urdu: تم نے علمت کی اقسام کو اجمالی جان لیا تو اب ہم مصنف کی بیان کردہ تقسیم کے مطابق علت کی ترجمہ تشریح اقسام بیان کرتے ہیں پس ہم کہتے ہیں کہ الأول علت اسماً ومعنى وحکماً کالبيع المطلق للملك الخالي عن خيار الشرط فإنه علت اسما لانه موضوع للملك والملك مضاف إليه ومعنى لانه يؤثر فيه وهو مشروع لاجله وحكما لانه يثبت الملك عند وجوده بلا تأخر. والثاني علت اسما لا حکما ولا معنى كالايجاب المعلق بالشرط وهو الذي ادخله فيما سبق في السبب المجازي مثل قوله انت طالق إن دخلت الدار فإن قوله انت طالق علت اسما لوقوع الطلاق فإنه موضوع له في الشرع ويضاف الحكم إليه عند وجود الشرط وليس علت حكما لان حكمه يتأخر إلى وجود الشرط ولا معنى اذ لا تأثير له فيه قبل وجود الشرط ومن هذا القبيل اليمين بالله تعالى للكفارة على ما قالوا. والثالث علت اسما ومعنى لا حكما كالبيع بشرط الخيار فإنه علت للملك اسما ومعنى لا حكما لتأخر الملك إلى اسقاط الخيار والبيع الموقف عطف عليه وهو مثال ثان والايجاب المضاف إلى وقت مثال ثالث كقوله انت طالق غدا ونصاب الزكاة قبل الحول مثال رابع.]
And when you know this, then now we proceed according to the division of the author and we say: The first: a cause in name, meaning, and ruling, like an absolute sale for ownership free from the option of condition. For it is a cause in name because it is set for ownership and ownership is attributed to it; in meaning because it has an effect in it and it is legislated for its sake; and in ruling because ownership is established at its occurrence without delay. The second: a cause in name only, not in ruling nor meaning, like an obligation suspended on a condition, and that is what he included previously in the metaphorical cause, such as his saying 'You are divorced if you enter the house'. For his saying 'You are divorced' is a cause in name for the occurrence of divorce, for it is set for it in law and the ruling is attributed to it upon occurrence of the condition; it is not a cause in ruling because its ruling is delayed until the condition exists, nor in meaning since there is no effect in it before the condition exists. Of this category is the oath by Allah for expiation according to what they said. The third: a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, like a sale with the option of condition. For it is a cause for ownership in name and meaning, not in ruling because ownership is delayed until the dropping of the option. The suspended sale is an appendage to it and is a second example; the obligation added to a time is a third example, like his saying 'You are divorced tomorrow'; and the nisab of zakat before the passing of a year is a fourth example.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Anwar, a commentary on Nur al-Anwar, expands on the classification of 'illah (cause) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author, after summarizing the logical possibilities (seven combinations of name, meaning, and ruling), follows the division of the original matn author into three categories. The first is an 'illah that is complete in all three aspects: it has the name of cause, it has a meaningful effect (ta'thir), and its legal consequence (hukm) occurs immediately. Example: an absolute sale without option — it is called a cause, it effects transfer of ownership, and ownership transfers instantly. The second is an 'illah only in name: it is called a cause but neither effects the ruling nor has a real effect until a condition is met. Example: a suspended pronouncement of divorce ('You are divorced if you enter the house'). The utterance is the cause for divorce but the effect and ruling are postponed until the condition. Similarly, an oath by Allah is a name for cause but the expiation is not obligatory until violation. The third is an 'illah in name and meaning but not in ruling: it has the name and the effect, but the ruling is delayed. Examples: a sale with an option (ownership transfers only after option is dropped), a suspended sale (ownership transfers only after owner's permission), a time-related pronouncement ('You are divorced tomorrow'), and zakat nisab before the year ends (obligation of payment is delayed). This classification is crucial for understanding how different types of causes operate in Hanafi law — some immediately binding, some delayed, some merely nominal.

Key Terms
عِلَّة (ʿillah): A cause that is set as a sign or means for a legal ruling; it may be considered in terms of name (ism), meaning (maʿnā), and ruling (ḥukm).
اسْمًا (asman): In name: the cause is linguistically or legally named as such, regardless of its actual effect.
مَعْنًى (maʿnan): In meaning: the cause has a real effect (ta'thir) on the ruling, even if the ruling is delayed.
حُكْمًا (ḥukman): In ruling: the legal consequence occurs immediately at the time of the cause's existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a cause that is a cause in name (asman) only, not in meaning or ruling?
A) An absolute sale
B) A suspended divorce (e.g., 'You are divorced if you enter the house')
C) A sale with the option of condition
D) The nisab of zakat before the year ends

Key Takeaway
A cause in Hanafi usul can be classified by its name, meaning, and ruling; only a full cause has immediate effect.

The First Type of 'Illah: Cause in Name, Meaning, and Ruling
Definition of the first type of 'illah with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the first type of 'illah in Hanafi usul and explain its three aspects (isman, ma'nan, hukman) using the example of absolute sale.

Core Idea
The strongest 'illah is effective in name, meaning, and ruling—ownership follows immediately upon an unconditional sale.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the 'illah (cause) is the attribute that connects a ruling to an act or object. This lesson introduces the strongest and most complete type of 'illah: one that qualifies as a cause in name, in meaning, and in ruling. The example of an absolute sale (bay' mutlaq) shows how the cause triggers immediate ownership without delay.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the three aspects
Identify the three dimensions: 'illatun isman (cause in name), 'illatun ma'nan (cause in meaning), and 'illatun hukman (cause in ruling). Understand each dimension separately before applying them to the example.
These terms are standard in Hanafi usul.
2
Analyze the example: absolute sale
The absolute sale (bay' mutlaq) is defined as a sale free from khiyar al-shart (option of condition). This sale is placed by the Shari' for ownership. Link each aspect of the 'illah to the sale: in name because the sale is designated for ownership; in meaning because it effectively transfers ownership; in ruling because ownership is immediate.
The condition of freedom from khiyar is crucial—it distinguishes this complete cause from weaker ones.
3
Generalize the concept
Any cause that fulfills all three aspects is a tammah (complete) 'illah. Consider other acts like marriage or divorce: they may also be complete causes if they meet the same criteria.
This classification helps in deriving rulings consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْأَوَّلُ عِلْةُ إِسْمَا وَمَعْنَى وَحُكْمَا كَالْبَيْعِ الْمُطْلَقِ الْمُفِيدِ لِلْمِلْكِ وَهُوَ الْبَيْعُ الَّذِي يَخْلُو عَنْ خِيَارِ الشَّرْطِ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لِأَنَّ الْبَيْعَ وُضِعَ لِلْمِلْكِ وَثُبُوتُ الْمِلْكِ يُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ وَمَعْنَى لِأَنَّهُ مُؤَثِّرٌ فِي ثُبُوتِ الْمِلْكِ وَحُكْمًا لِأَنَّهُ عِنْدَ وُجُودِهِ يَحْصُلُ الْمِلْكُ بِلَا تَأْخِيرٍ
The first is a cause in name, meaning, and ruling, like the absolute sale that benefits ownership, and it is the sale that is free from the option of condition. For it is a cause in name because the sale was placed for ownership, and the establishment of ownership is attributed to it; and in meaning because it is effective in establishing ownership; and in ruling because at its occurrence ownership is obtained without delay.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines the first of four types of 'illah (cause) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It is a cause that qualifies in three aspects: name (ism), meaning (ma'na), and ruling (hukm). The example given is an absolute sale (bay' mutlaq), i.e., a sale contract with no option to rescind (khiyar al-shart). Such a sale is called a cause in name because the lawgiver (shari') designated it for transferring ownership; in meaning because it truly effects that transfer; and in ruling because ownership is immediate without being delayed to a future condition.

Key Terms
عِلَّةُ اسْمًا ('illatun isman): The cause is attributed to the ruling in name because the shar'i designated it for that ruling.
عِلَّةُ مَعْنَى ('illatun ma'nan): The cause is genuinely effective (mu'aththir) in producing the ruling.
عِلَّةُ حُكْمًا ('illatun hukman): The ruling is immediately attached to the cause without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is effective in transferring ownership
C) Because ownership is obtained immediately without delay
D) Because it is free from option of condition

Key Takeaway
An absolute sale free from khiyar al-shart exemplifies the strongest 'illah: one that is a cause in name, meaning, and ruling, producing immediate ownership.

The 'Illah that is a Cause in Name Only
Classifying causes that are effective in name/meaning but not in ruling due to delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify an 'illah that is effective in name and meaning but not in ruling, and distinguish it from a fully effective cause.

Core Idea
A cause can be valid in name and meaning yet its legal ruling is delayed to a future condition or time.

This passage introduces a second type of 'illah: one that is a cause in name and meaning but not in ruling. The author provides five examples from fiqh — conditional talāq, suspended sale, future divorce, zakat nisab, and hire contracts — each illustrating how a legal cause can be present and effective yet the ruling it produces is postponed. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the Hanafi theory of causation to rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three attributes of a cause (illah)
Recognize that a cause can be evaluated in terms of name (subject matter), meaning (real effect), and ruling (immediate legal result). This passage categorizes causes that have name and possibly meaning but lack the ruling attribute.
The classification is based on the presence or absence of these three aspects.
2
Examine the first example: conditional divorce
The phrase 'You are divorced if you enter the house' is a cause in name because the words are for divorce, but it has no meaning before the condition occurs and thus no ruling.
This example is unique in lacking meaning as well.
3
Examine the second example: suspended sale (bay' mawquf)
A sale that depends on authorization (e.g., selling someone else's property) is a valid sale in name and meaning, but ownership transfers only upon authorization, so it is not a cause in ruling.
This is the paradigmatic example of a cause with meaning but delayed ruling.
4
Examine the remaining examples: future divorce, nisab, ijarah
Each of these has a cause that is real in subject and effect but the ruling is deferred: future divorce to the specified time, nisab zakat to the end of the year, and ijarah to the gradual delivery of usufruct.
All share the feature of ta'khir (delay).
5
Synthesize the common 'illa: the ruling is delayed due to a condition or time
Conclude that the absence of immediate ruling does not negate the causal relationship; it merely indicates the presence of a suspending factor (condition or time).
This is a key nuance in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالثَّانِى عِلَّةٌ اسمًا لا حكماً ولا معنى كَقَوْلِهِ انتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ دَخَلْتِ الدَّارَ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لِأَنَّهُ وُضِعَ لِلطَّلَاقِ لَا حُكْمًا لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ مُؤَخَّرٌ إِلَى وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ وَلَا مَعْنَى لِأَنَّهُ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهُ قَبْلَ الشَّرْطِ وَالْبَيْعُ الْمَوْقُوفُ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا وَمَعْنَى لَا حُكْمًا لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ يَثْبُتُ عِنْدَ الْإِجَازَةِ وَالْإِنْجَازُ الْمُضَافُ إِلَى وَقْتٍ كَقَوْلِهِ انتِ طَالِقٌ غَدًا فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا وَمَعْنَى لَا حُكْمًا لِلتَّأْخِيرِ وَنِصَابُ الزَّكَاةِ قَبْلَ مَضِيِّ الْحَوْلِ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا وَمَعْنَى لَا حُكْمًا لِتَأَخُّرِ الْأَدَاءِ إِلَى الْحَوْلِ وَعَقْدُ الْإِجَارَةِ مِثَالٌ خَامِسٌ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لِمِلْكِ الْمَنْفَعَةِ اسْمًا وَمَعْنَى لَا حُكْمًا لِأَنَّ مِلْكَ الْمَنَافِعِ يَتَجَدَّدُ شَيْئًا فَشَيْئًا
The second is a cause in name only, not in ruling nor meaning, like his saying 'You are divorced if you enter the house.' For it is a cause in name because it was placed for divorce, not in ruling because the ruling is delayed until the condition occurs, and not in meaning because it has no effect before the condition. And the suspended sale (al-bay' al-mawquf): it is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, because ownership is established upon authorization. And the immediate act attached to a time like saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' — it is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, due to delay. And the nisab of zakat before the passage of the year — it is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, because the obligation of payment is delayed until the year. And the contract of hire is a fifth example: it is a cause for ownership of usufruct in name and meaning, not in ruling, because ownership of usufruct is renewed gradually.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage covers the second type of 'illah, which is a cause in name only (or in name and meaning but not in ruling). The first example is a conditional divorce (ta'liq): the statement 'You are divorced if you enter the house' is a cause in name because the words are designed for divorce, but neither effective nor immediate. The second example is a suspended sale (bay' mawquf, e.g., selling another's property without permission); it is a cause in name and meaning (it is genuinely a sale and effective once authorized) but not in ruling because ownership is deferred. The third is adding a future time to an divorce pronouncement (e.g., 'you are divorced tomorrow'); same logic. The fourth is the nisab (minimum threshold) for zakat before the Islamic year passes; it is a cause in name and meaning (wealth is present and effective in requiring charity) but not in ruling because payment is only due after a full year. The fifth is a hire contract (ijarah); it is a cause for the ownership of usufruct in name and meaning, but the ruling (ownership) occurs gradually over time, so it is not immediate. Thus none of these are causes in ruling (hukman) because the legal effect is postponed.

Key Terms
التَّعْلِيق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a legal ruling on a future condition.
الْبَيْعُ الْمَوْقُوفُ (al-bay' al-mawquf): A sale that is not final pending authorization.
نِصَابُ الزَّكَاةِ (nisab al-zakat): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers zakat obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a suspended sale (bay' mawquf) a cause in name and meaning but not in ruling?
A) Because it is not a valid sale
B) Because ownership is deferred until authorization
C) Because it has no effect at all
D) Because the sale is conditional

Key Takeaway
A cause can be valid in name and meaning even if its legal ruling is delayed; the delay does not negate its causal character.

The Fourth Type of 'Illah: 'Illat al-'Illah (Cause of the Cause)
Explaining the fourth type of 'illah as cause of the cause with three examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case of 'illat al-'illah, distinguish it from a direct cause (sabab), and apply the Hanafi ruling on retraction of witnesses.

Core Idea
A cause can be effective through an intermediary; such a cause ('illat al-'illah) resembles a sabab in mediating the ruling.

In Hanafi usul, causes (ilal) are classified in various ways. The fourth type is the 'cause of the cause'—a cause that produces its effect only through an intermediate cause. This lesson explores its definition through three examples and a fiqh dispute that hinges on this classification, showing how the identification of the 'illah directly impacts legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'illat al-'illah
Read the Arabic definition: it is a cause that leads to another cause, which then directly produces the ruling. It is termed 'illat al-'illah (cause of the cause) and resembles a sabab (direct cause) because it mediates between the initial factor and the ruling.
2
Analyze the first example: purchase of a relative
The purchase of a relative (who is a slave) causes ownership; ownership then causes emancipation. Thus, the purchase is the cause of the cause of emancipation. Note how it is both a cause (because it initiates the chain) and similar to a sabab (because it mediates).
This example illustrates the dual nature: cause in essence, but mediator in operation.
3
Analyze the second example: deathbed illness
Deathbed illness (marad al-mawt) causes the attachment of heirs' rights to the property; that attachment then restricts the patient from donating more than one-third. Some scholars argue it is a cause in name and meaning but not in ruling because the restriction only takes effect upon death, not during the illness itself.
The nuance: the illness is effective (athar) but the ruling (hukm) is contingent on death.
4
Analyze the third example: tazkiya and the dispute on retraction
Tazkiya (validation of witnesses) is a cause for the acceptance of testimony; testimony is a cause for stoning. Hence tazkiya is a cause of the cause. Abu Hanifa holds that if the testifiers retract after stoning, they must pay blood money (diyah) because their tazkiya is the cause of the stoning. The two disciples (Sahibayn) disagree, arguing they only praised the witnesses and are not connected to imposing the hadd.
This dispute directly shows how the classification of 'illah affects legal liability.
5
Evaluate the consequence of the dispute
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is the payment of blood money upon retraction. Understanding the 'illat al-'illah concept helps explain why Abu Hanifa imposes liability: the tazkiya is an indirect but real cause. The Sahibayn's view treats it as mere praise, not a cause.
Even though the text does not give a tarjih, the dispute is a classic illustration of how usul categories shape fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالرَّابِعُ عِلَّةٌ فِي خَيْرِ الأَسْبَابِ يَعْنِي لَهَا شَبَهٌ بِالأَسْبَابِ فَهُوَ تَفْسِيرٌ لِمَا قَبْلَهُ وَذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ لَهُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَمْثِلَةٍ فَقَالَ كَشِرَاءِ الْقَرِيبِ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لِلْمِلْكِ وَالْمِلْكُ عِلَّةٌ لِلْعِتْقِ فَيَكُونُ الْعِتْقُ مُضَافًا إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِوَاسِطَتِهِ فَمِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ عِلَّةُ الْعِلَّةِ كَانَ عِلَّةً وَمِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ تَوَسَّطَ بَيْنَهُمَا كَانَ شَبِيهًا بِالأَسْبَابِ وَمَرَضِ الْمَوْتِ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لِتَعَلُّقِ حَقِّ الْوَرَثَةِ بِالْمَالِ وَهُوَ عِلَّةٌ لِحَجْرِ الْمَرِيضِ عَنِ التَّبَرُّعِ بِمَا زَادَ عَلَى الثُّلُثِ فَيَكُونُ كَشِرَاءِ الْقَرِيبِ وَرُبَّمَا يُقَالُ إِنَّهُ دَاخِلٌ فِي الْعِلَّةِ اسْمًا وَمَعْنَى لَا حُكْمًا فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ اسْمًا لِحَجْرِ الْمَرِيضِ عَنِ التَّبَرُّعَاتِ لِإِضَافَةِ الْحُكْمُ إِلَيْهِ وَمَعْنَى لِكَوْنِهِ مُؤَثِّرًا لِكَوْنِهِ فِي الْحَجْرِ لَا حُكْمًا لِأَنَّ الْحَجْرَ لَا يَثْبُتُ إِلَّا إِذَا اتَّصَلَ بِهِ الْمَوْتُ وَالتَّزْكِيَةُ اتَّصَلَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ فَإِنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لِلشَّهَادَةِ وَهِيَ عِلَّةٌ لِلرَّجْمِ فَتَكُونُ عِلَّةَ الْعِلَّةِ كَشِرَاءِ الْقَرِيبِ فَلَوْ رَجَعَ الْمَزْكُونَ بَعْدَ الرَّجْمِ يَضْمَنُونَ الدِّيَةَ عِنْدَهُ وَعِنْدَهُمَا لَا يَضْمَنُونَ لِأَنَّهُمْ أَثْنَوْا عَلَى الشُّهُودِ خَيْرًا وَلَا تَعَلَّقَ لَهُمْ بِإِيجَابِ الْحَدِّ فَصَارُوا كَمَا لَوْ أَثْنَوْا عَلَى الْمَشْهُودِ لَهُ خَيْرًا بِأَنْ قَالُوا هُوَ مُحْصَنٌ ثُمَّ رَجَعُوا فَكَذَا هَذَا
The fourth is a cause in the sense of being like a cause (sabab), meaning it has resemblance to causes. This is an explanation of what preceded. The author mentioned three examples: (1) The purchase of a relative: it is a cause for ownership, and ownership is a cause for emancipation, so emancipation is attributed to the first through it. Insofar as it is the cause of the cause, it is a cause; and insofar as it mediates between them, it is similar to a cause. (2) Deathbed illness: it is a cause for the attachment of the heirs' right to the property, and that right is a cause for restricting the patient from donating more than one-third, so it is like the purchase of a relative. Some say it falls under the cause in name and meaning, not in ruling: it is a cause in name for restricting the patient from donations because the ruling is attributed to it; in meaning because it is effective in the restriction; not in ruling because the restriction is only established when death is connected. (3) Testimony (tazkiya) according to Abu Hanifa: it is a cause for testimony, and testimony is a cause for stoning, so it is a cause of the cause like the purchase of a relative. If the testifiers retract after stoning, they must pay blood money according to him, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they do not, because they praised the witnesses well and they are not connected to imposing the hadd penalty, so they become like those who praised the person testified about by saying 'he is muhsan' and then retracted.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth type of 'illah, which is a cause that is itself the cause of another cause (illat al-illah), resembling a direct sabab. The author gives three examples. First, purchasing a relative who is a slave: the purchase causes ownership, and ownership causes emancipation (by law in some cases). Thus the purchase is a cause indirectly; it is called a 'cause of the cause'. Second, deathbed illness (marad al-mawt): the illness attaches the heirs' right to the property, and that attachment restricts the patient from giving away more than one-third in charity. The illness is a cause of the restriction indirectly. Some scholars consider this illness to be a cause in name and meaning but not in ruling because the restriction only takes effect upon death. Third, tazkiya (testimony of witnesses to establish adultery) according to Abu Hanifa: the witnesses' statements (tazkiya) cause the testimony to be accepted, and the testimony causes the stoning penalty. So the tazkiya is a cause of the cause. A subsidiary ruling: if the witnesses retract after the stoning, Abu Hanifa says they must pay blood money, while the two disciples (Sahibayn) say they do not, because they only praised the witnesses and are not directly responsible for the hadd penalty. This illustrates how the classification of 'illah can affect fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
عِلَّةُ الْعِلَّةِ ('illat al-'illah): A cause that leads to another cause which directly produces the ruling, thus it is an indirect cause.
شِبْه السَّبَب (shibh al-sabab): Resembling a direct cause because it mediates between the first cause and the ruling.
التَّزْكِيَة (al-tazkiya): The act of witnesses giving testimony that establishes the conditions for a penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of 'illat al-'illah that leads to a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn?
A) Purchase of a relative
B) Deathbed illness
C) Tazkiya (witness validation)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The 'illat al-'illah is a cause that operates through an intermediary, and its correct identification can lead to different fiqh outcomes, as seen in the dispute over witness retraction.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
6 min
Definition and Types of Shart in Hanafi Usul
Defining shart and classifying its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shart in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguish it from illa and juz, and classify the five types of shart with examples.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) affects existence but not obligation; it is extrinsic to the essence.

In Hanafi usul, understanding the adjuncts of rulings (muta'alliqat al-ahkam) is crucial for deriving law. The condition (shart) is one such adjunct, often confused with cause (illa) or part (juz). This lesson clarifies its precise definition, the essential distinction from illa, and the five types identified by induction, with concrete fiqh examples to illustrate each.

Guided Path
1
1. Grasp the definition of shart
Analyze the definition: shart is that upon which the existence of a ruling depends, but not its obligation. It must be extrinsic to the essence of the thing (to exclude parts). Compare with illa (which affects both existence and obligation) and juz (which is intrinsic).
The addition 'kharijan an mahiyatihi' is critical to avoid confusing a part with a condition.
2
2. Recognize the five-fold classification by induction
The text states that shart is divided into five types based on investigation (istiqra'). The first two are fully explained: pure condition (shart mahd) and condition in the status of a cause (shart fi hukm al-illa).
The remaining three are not detailed here but are introduced; focus on the logic behind the classification.
3
3. Analyze the pure condition (shart mahd)
This condition has no effect on the ruling itself; it is merely a prerequisite for the cause to operate. Example: a suspended divorce ('If you enter the house, you are divorced') – entry is a pure condition for divorce to occur.
The cause of divorce is the husband's statement; entry only triggers it.
4
4. Analyze the condition in the status of a cause (shart fi hukm al-illa)
This condition is treated like a cause for liability, even though the real cause is different. Example: digging a well in a public road – the real cause of falling is gravity, but digging removes the preventer (ground), so the digger is liable for damages if the well is on another's property.
Liability arises only if the condition is created without right; digging on one's own property or intentional jumping negates liability.
5
5. Note the third type and intra-Hanafi difference
The third type (shart lahu hukm al-asbab) involves an intervening act of a free agent where the act is not attributed to the condition. Example: opening a birdcage – Muhammad holds the opener liable for the bird's escape, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree.
This shows how classification affects practical legal outcomes.

مانع اور روکنے والی تھی ۔ اور کنویں کا كود تا گویا مائع عن سقوط الى الثقل کو زائل کر دنیا ہے اور موانع سقوط کو دور کر دیتا ہے وجود شرط کے درجہ میں اور مشی (یعنی کنویں کے پاس چل کر جانا) سب محض ہے اس لئے اس کا پھاڑ دیا کا جانا شرط ہے کیونکہ مشکیزہ پہنے سے مانع تھا اور اس مائع کا زائل کر دینا مافیہ کے بہنے کے لئے شرط ہے اور علت اس میں مافیہ کا سیال پتلا پانی کی طرح ہوتا ہے اس کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا وَبَيْنَ الْمَشْرُوطِ فِعْلُ فَاعِلٍ مُختارِ لا يَكُونُ ذَالِكَ الْفِعْلُ مَنْسُوبًا إِلَى ذَالِكَ الشَّرْطِ وَيَكُونَ ذَالِكَ الشرط سابقا على ذالِكَ الْفِعْلِ وَاحْتَرَز به عَمَّا إِذَا تَخَلَّلَ فِعْلُ فَاعِلٍ طَبِيعِي كَحَفْرِ الْبِيْرِ فَإِنَّهُ فِي حُكْمِ الْعِلَلِ وَعَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ ذَالِكَ الْفِعْلُ مَنْسوبًا إِلَى ذَالِك الشَّرْطِ كَفَتْحِ بَابٍ قَفْص الطَّيْرِ إِذْ طَيْرَانُهُ مَنْسُوبٌ إِلَى الْفَتْحِ فَإِنَّهُ أَيْضًا فِي حكم الْعِلَلِ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٌ حَتَّى يَضمِنَ الفاتِحُ عِنْدَهُ خِلَافًا لَهُمَا وَأَمَّا اذَالَمْ يَكُن
The assembly because it is similar to giving choice, and choice is restricted to the assembly. And the third: the condition (shart), which is that upon which existence depends without obligation. This distinguishes it from the cause (illa). It is appropriate to add to it his saying: 'and it is outside its essence' to exclude the part. Thus it has been said, and by induction it is five. The first is a pure condition (shart mahd) which has no effect on the ruling, but rather the existence of the cause depends on it, like entering the house in relation to the occurrence of the conditional divorce in his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The second is a condition that is in the status of the cause (shart fi hukm al-illa) regarding the attribution of the ruling to it and the obligation of compensation (daman) on its doer, like digging a well in the road. For it is a condition for the destruction of what is destroyed by falling into it, because the real cause is the heavy object due to the inclination of the heavy nature towards falling, but the ground was a preventing, holding force. Digging the well is removal of the preventer, and removal of the preventer is of the category of conditions. Walking is a pure cause (sabab mahd), not a cause (illa) for it. Thus digging, which is the condition, is made to stand in the place of the cause regarding compensation if he digs in another's property. As for if he digs in his own property or a person throws himself intentionally into the well, then there is no compensation on the digger at all. And ripping the waterskin, for it is a condition for the flowing out of what is inside it, because the waterskin was a preventer and its removal is a condition, and the cause is that what is inside is flowing, but it is not suitable for the ruling to be attributed to it, as it is a thing created upon that; so it is attributed to the condition, and the owner of the condition is liable for the destruction of what is inside and also for the deficiency of burning. And among the examples of establishing the proof in place of causes: making the report of love stand in the place of love. For example, a man says to his wife: 'If you love me, you are divorced,' and she says, 'I love you,' then divorce occurs upon her because love is an internal quality and knowledge of it can only occur through report. But this ruling is specific to the assembly because this speech resembles giving choice, and choice is specific to the assembly in which choice is given. Types of condition: And the third is the condition, which is that upon which existence depends. And the third type of the adjuncts of rulings is the condition, and the condition is called that upon which the existence of something is attached but not its obligation. The phrase 'without obligation' distinguishes it from the cause (because obligation is attached to the cause). And it is appropriate that in this definition the words 'and it is outside its essence' be added so that the part is excluded from the definition. Some have said similarly. And it is five, limited to five by induction after investigation. The first is a pure condition (shart mahd): a condition that has no effect in the ruling, but rather the existence of the cause depends on it, like entering the house in relation to the occurrence of divorce, the divorce that is suspended on entering the house, in his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The second is a condition that is in the status of the cause (shart fi hukm al-illa): a condition that is like a cause in the ruling, meaning the ruling is attributed to it like a cause, and compensation is obligatory on its doer, like digging a well in the road. For it is a condition for the destruction of what is destroyed by falling into it, because the real cause is the heavy object (weight) because the nature of the heavy thing is inclined towards falling, but the ground was a preventer and holder. Digging the well is removal of the preventer, and removal of the preventer is of the category of conditions. Walking (going near the well) is a pure cause, not a cause for it. So digging (the well) which is actually a condition is made to stand in the place of a cause regarding the obligation of compensation when the well is dug in another's property. And if the well is dug in his own property or someone throws himself intentionally into the well, then there is no compensation on the digger at all. And ripping the waterskin: because ripping it is a condition for the flowing out of what is inside it, because the waterskin was a preventer and its removal is a condition. The cause is that what is inside is flowing liquid like water; it is not suitable for the ruling to be attributed to it (i.e., because it is thin and flowing, it flows due to its natural inclination, so the flowing was created upon it, therefore the ruling is attributed to the condition). The owner of the condition (the ripper) will be liable for the destruction of what is inside and also for the ripping of the waterskin. The third is a condition that has the status of causes (shart lahu hukm al-asbab). It is the condition in between which and the conditioned an act of a free agent intervenes, and that act is not attributed to that condition, and that condition precedes that act. This distinguishes it from when the act of a natural agent intervenes, like digging a well, for that is in the status of causes, and from when that act is attributed to that condition, like opening the door of a birdcage, because the bird's flying is attributed to the opening; for that also is in the status of causes according to Muhammad, so that the opener is liable according to him, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani?). And as for when it is not...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Anwar, a commentary on Nur al-Anwar, defines the term 'shart' (condition) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and begins classifying its types. The author first distinguishes shart from 'illa' (cause): a shart is something on which the existence of a ruling depends but not its obligation (wujub). The cause (illa) is what necessitates the ruling itself. The author also adds that a shart must be extrinsic to the essence of the thing, to exclude parts (juz') of the thing from being considered conditions. By induction (istiqra'), shart is divided into five types, though only the first two are fully explained here. The first type is 'shart mahd' (pure condition), which has no effect on the ruling itself but is merely a prerequisite for the cause to operate. An example from Hanafi fiqh is a suspended divorce: if a man says to his wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' entering the house is a pure condition for the divorce to occur; the actual cause of divorce is the husband's statement or intention, but the condition (entry) must exist for the effect to take place. The second type is a condition that is treated like a cause regarding the attribution of the ruling and liability (daman). The classic example is digging a well in a public road. The real cause of an object falling into the well is the object's weight and gravity, but the ground prevented it. Digging removes this preventer, so it is a condition for the fall. However, Hanafi law holds the digger liable for damages caused by the well (if dug in another's property) because the condition is elevated to the status of a cause for liability. If the well is dug in one's own property, or if someone deliberately jumps in, the diger is not liable. Similarly, ripping a waterskin: the ripping is a condition for the liquid to flow; the liquid's nature is the cause of flowing, but since the liquid itself cannot be attributed with the act, liability falls on the one who rips. The passage also includes an Urdu commentary explaining that the report of love (ikhbar) can take the place of actual love in a suspended divorce, but only if the statement is made in the same assembly, analogous to giving an option (takhyir). The third type of shart (shart lahu hukm al-asbab) is introduced but not completed in these pages. It is a condition that involves an intervening act of a free agent, where the act is not attributed to the condition. This distinguishes it from cases like digging a well (natural agent) or opening a birdcage (where the act is attributed to the condition, leading to liability according to Imam Muhammad but not according to the two companions).

Key Terms
الشَّرْط (al-shart): That upon which the existence of something depends, but not its obligation; and it is outside the essence of that thing.
الْعِلَّة (al-illa): That upon which both the existence and obligation of a ruling depend; it is the effective cause that necessitates the ruling.
الْمَانِع (al-mani'): A preventer that blocks the effect of a cause; removal of the preventer is a condition (shart) for the cause to operate.
شَرْط مَحْض (shart mahd): A pure condition that has no effect on the ruling itself; it is merely a prerequisite for the cause to operate.
شَرْط فِي حُكْم الْعِلَّة (shart fi hukm al-illa): A condition that is treated like a cause regarding the attribution of the ruling and liability, even though the real cause is something else.
شَرْط لَهُ حُكْمُ الْأَسْبَاب (shart lahu hukm al-asbab): A condition in between which and the conditioned an act of a free agent intervenes, where that act is not attributed to the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of digging a well in a public road, why is the digger held liable for damages even though the real cause of falling is gravity?
A) Because digging a well is a prohibited act.
B) Because the digger's action is the real cause (illa) of the fall.
C) Because digging removes the preventer (ground) and is treated as a condition in the status of a cause (shart fi hukm al-illa).
D) Because the well is dug on another's property.

Key Takeaway
Shart is an extrinsic condition for existence, not obligation, and is classified into five types; the distinction between shart mahd and shart fi hukm al-illa is crucial for determining liability.

Distinguishing Shart, Ma'nā al-Shart, and 'Alāmah in Hanafi Uṣūl
Defining sharṭ types and a rule for distinguishing sharṭ and ma‘nā al-sharṭ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of shart in Hanafi usul and apply the rule (ḍābiṭa) that distinguishes a true condition (shart) from a condition by description (ma‘nā al-shart) and from a sign (‘alāmah).

Core Idea
A condition is known by its explicit particle; a description functions as a condition only when attached to an indefinite, absent referent.

In Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh, the term 'sharṭ' (condition) can refer to several distinct concepts: the explicit conditional clause (e.g., 'if you enter'), a description that effectively serves as a condition (e.g., 'whom I marry'), and a sign (e.g., iḥsān) that indicates the applicable ruling without being a condition. This passage from a classical commentary clarifies these categories and provides the precise rule (ḍābiṭa) for when a description counts as a condition versus when it is superfluous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories mentioned
The passage discusses: (1) sharṭ by explicit formula (ṣīgha), (2) what is in the meaning of a condition (mā fī ma‘nā al-sharṭ), and (3) the sign (‘alāmah). First, locate each term in the Arabic text and note the examples given.
The numbering in the OCR may be off; the passage likely refers to the fourth or fifth type of condition, but the focus is on the conceptual distinction.
2
Analyze the rule (ḍābiṭa) for distinguishing sharṭ from mā fī ma‘nā al-sharṭ
The author states that a true condition is known by its explicit particle (e.g., 'in'). For mā fī ma‘nā al-sharṭ, the key is that the condition-like description appears in an indefinite noun (nakira) referring to an absent (ghā’ib) entity. Apply this: if the noun is definite (mu‘ayyan) by pointing, the description is superfluous and does not create a condition.
The indefinite here is not grammatical indefiniteness; the noun can be definite with lam but unspecified by pointing.
3
Distinguish the sign (‘alāmah) from sharṭ and other categories
The passage defines ‘alāmah as something that makes existence known without being necessary for the existence of the ruling or attached to an obligation. Insertion of iḥsān as an example: it does not cause stoning but indicates that stoning is the punishment once zina is proven.
This contrasts with sabab (cause), which requires the effect, and ‘illah (effective cause), which is attached to obligation.
4
Apply the distinction to the given examples
The passage gives two examples: (a) 'The woman whom I marry is divorced three times' – here 'whom I marry' is mā fī ma‘nā al-sharṭ because the woman is unspecified. (b) 'This woman, whom I marry, is divorced' – here the description is useless because the woman is pointed to directly. Explain why each case is categorized as such.
The rule hinges on whether the description adds new information or is redundant.

Source Text

☁ والخامس شرط هو كالعلامة. ثم إنهم يهتدي ضابطة يعرف بها الفرق بين الشرط وما في معناه على ما قال: وإنما يعرف الشرط بصيغته كحروف الشرط مثل قوله إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق. وفيه تنبيه على أن صيغة الشرط لا ينفك عن معنى الشرط قط. أو دلالته وهي الوصف الذي يكون في معنى الشرط كقوله المرأة التي أتزوجها طالق ثلاثاً فإنه بمعنى الشرط دلالة لوقوع الوصف في النكرة (أي المرأة الغير المعينة بالإشارة لا النكرة النحوية إذ هي معرفة باللام). فلما دخل وصف التزوج في المنكرة وهو معتبر في الغائب يصلح دلالة على الشرط فصار كأنه قال إن تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق. ولو وقع في المعين بأن يقول هذه المرأة التي أتزوج فهي طالق لم يصلح دلالة على الشرط لأن الوصف في الحاضر لغو لأن الإشارة أبلغ في التعريف من الوصف فكأنه قال هذه المرأة طالق فيلغو. والرابع العلامة وهي ما يعرف الوجود من غير أن يتعلق به وجوب لا وجود. فقوله ما يعرف الوجود احتراز عن السبب إذ هو مقتض لا معرف. وقوله من غير أن يتعلق به وجوب احتراز عن العلة. ولا وجود احتراز عن الشرط. كالإحصان في باب الزنا فإنه علامة للرجم وهو عبارة عن كون الزاني حراً مسلما مكلفا وطئ بنكاح صحيح مرة. فالتكليف شرط في سائر الأحكام والحرية لتكمل العقوبة وإنما العدة هنا هي الإسلام والوطء بالنكاح الصحيح. وإنما جعلناه علامة لا شرطاً لأن الزنا إذا تحقق لا يتوقف انعقاده علة للرجم على إحصان يحدث بعده. إذ لو وجد الإحصان بعد الزنا
And the fifth is a condition that is like a sign. Then indeed they come with a rule by which the difference between a condition and what is in its meaning is known, as he said: And the condition is known by its formula, like the particles of condition, such as his saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' And in it is a warning that the formula of condition is never separated from the meaning of condition. Or its indication, and it is the description that is in the meaning of the condition, like his saying 'The woman whom I marry is divorced three times,' for it is in the meaning of condition by indication because the description occurs in the indefinite (meaning the woman not specified by pointing, not the grammatical indefinite, for it is definite with the lam). So when the description of marriage entered into the indefinite, and it is considered in the absent, it is suitable as an indication of the condition, so it becomes as if he said 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced.' And if it occurred in the definite, such as his saying 'This woman, whom I marry, is divorced,' it would not be suitable as an indication of the condition because the description in the present is superfluous, for pointing is more emphatic in definition than the description, so it is as if he said 'This woman is divorced,' so it becomes superfluous. And the fourth is the sign, and it is that by which existence is known without any obligation or existence being attached to it. So his saying 'that by which existence is known' is a precaution against the cause, for it is a requirer, not a definer. And his saying 'without any obligation being attached to it' is a precaution against the effective cause. And 'without existence' is a precaution against the condition. Like ihsan in the chapter of zina, for it is a sign for stoning, and it is an expression of the adulterer being free, Muslim, morally responsible, having had valid intercourse once. So moral responsibility is a condition in all rulings, and freedom is for completion of the punishment, but the mainstay here is Islam and intercourse in a valid marriage. And we made it a sign, not a condition, because when zina has occurred, its being a cause for stoning does not depend on ihsan occurring after it. For if ihsan occurred after zina
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory text from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary. It discusses two main topics: (1) the rule (dabit) for distinguishing between a true condition (shart) and that which is in the meaning of a condition (ma'na al-shart), and (2) the fifth type of condition, which is like a sign ('alamah).

The author first states that a condition (shart) is known by its explicit formula (sighah), such as the conditional particles like 'in' (if). The example given is from divorce: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The condition here is the entry, and its effect (divorce) is suspended upon it. The author emphasizes that the conditional particle is inseparable from the meaning of condition.

For ma'na al-shart, it is known by a description that effectively serves as a condition. For example, a man says: 'The woman whom I marry is divorced three times.' Although no explicit 'if' is used, the description 'whom I marry' functions as a condition because it is placed on an indefinite noun (al-mar'ah al-ghayr al-mu'ayyanah). The indefinite here means not specified by pointing (i.e., an unspecified woman), not the grammatical indefinite (since 'al-mar'ah' is definite with lam). Because the description 'tazawwaj' (marry) occurs in the indefinite and is considered in the absent (gha'ib), it indicates a condition. It becomes as if he said: 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced.' 

However, if the description occurs in a definite noun (e.g., 'this woman whom I marry'), it does not indicate a condition because the description is superfluous when the woman is already present and pointed to. Pointing is stronger in definition than description, so it is as if he directly said: 'This woman is divorced,' making the description meaningless.

The second part defines the fourth (or fifth, as earlier stated) type: al-'alamah (the sign). A sign is something that indicates existence without any obligation or existence being attached to it. This distinguishes it from sabab (cause), which requires the existence of the effect; from 'illah (effective cause), which is attached to an obligation; and from shart (condition), which is attached to existence (i.e., the existence of the condition is necessary for the existence of the effect). The example given is ihsan (being muhsan) in the case of zina (unlawful sexual intercourse). Ihsan is a sign for the punishment of stoning (rajm). Ihsan is defined as the adulterer being free, Muslim, morally responsible (mukallaf), and having had valid sexual intercourse in a valid marriage at least once. Among these, moral responsibility is a condition in all rulings, freedom is for the completion of punishment, but the main pillars here are Islam and intercourse in a valid marriage. The reason ihsan is considered a sign and not a condition for stoning is that once zina has already occurred, the applicability of stoning as a cause does not depend on ihsan occurring after the act; if ihsan occurs after zina, it does not change the punishment. Hence, ihsan is not a condition but a sign that indicates the severity of the offense.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that suspends a ruling upon its occurrence, known by its explicit particle (sighah) such as 'in'.
ما في معنى الشرط (ma fī ma'nā al-shart): A description that functions like a condition without an explicit particle, recognized by its occurrence in an indefinite noun for an absent referent.
علامة (ʿalāmah): A sign that indicates existence without any obligation or existence depending on it; it serves to identify the applicable ruling (e.g., ihsan indicates stoning).
ضابطة (ḍābiṭa): A rule that distinguishes between a condition (shart) and what is in its meaning (ma'na al-shart).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a description (waṣf) function as a condition (mā fī ma‘nā al-sharṭ)?
A) It is attached to a definite noun with a pointing reference.
B) It is attached to an indefinite noun referring to an absent entity.
C) It includes an explicit conditional particle like 'in'.
D) It describes a present, pointed-to individual.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a condition is either explicit by particle or implicit by description in an indefinite, absent referent; a sign indicates existence without being necessary for the ruling.

The 'Alamah (Sign) in Hanafi Usul: Definition and the Example of Ihsan
Defines 'alamah and illustrates with ihsan in zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'alamah (sign) in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from sabab, 'illah, and shart, and apply this distinction to the Hanafi analysis of ihsan in zina.

Core Idea
A sign indicates a ruling's existence without causing it — ihsan shows stoning is due but does not necessitate it.

This passage introduces the fourth type of legal connection in Hanafi usul: the 'alamah (sign). Unlike a cause (sabab), effective cause ('illah), or condition (shart), a sign merely points to the ruling without being a constituent element. The example of ihsan in the law of zina illustrates this and leads to an internal Hanafi debate. By the end, you will be able to define 'alamah, distinguish it from related concepts, and apply the distinction to the case of ihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four connections
The passage distinguishes 'alamah from sabab, 'illah, and shart. Note the boundaries: sabab necessitates, 'illah is the effective cause, shart is a condition on which the ruling depends, and 'alamah merely indicates.
These are the four key categories in Hanafi usul for linking rulings to their indicators.
2
Understand the definition of 'alamah
The passage defines 'alamah as 'that by which existence is known without any obligation or existence being attached to it.' This means the sign reveals the ruling's presence but does not cause or condition it.
The key phrase is 'without a requirement of obligation or existence being attached to it'.
3
Apply the definition to ihsan
Ihsan is described as the fornicator being free, Muslim, mukallaf, and having had valid intercourse. Some later Hanafis treat it as a sign for stoning because if someone becomes muhsan after committing zina, stoning is not applied.
This shows ihsan does not cause stoning; rather it describes the state in which zina warrants stoning.
4
Analyze the internal ikhtilaf
The majority of Hanafis consider ihsan a condition (shart) for stoning, arguing that stoning depends on it just as amputation depends on nisab. Later scholars consider it a sign. Understand each side's proof.
The passage says 'the choice of the majority is that it is a condition'.
5
Explore the practical thamarah
The passage gives a branch (furu'): if witnesses of ihsan retract after stoning, on the sign view they do not pay blood-money because a sign carries no obligation. This contrasts with witnesses of the act itself.
This practical consequence highlights the difference between sign and condition.
