And the rulings divided in this chapter, i.e., the chapter of establishing the validity of performance upon partial capacity without full capacity which was mentioned shortly ago, into six divisions. The author indicated them in order and said: 'So the right of Allah, if it is good with no possibility of other than it, like faith, it is necessary to say that it is valid from the minor without obligation of performance. This is the first division.' And we only said valid because Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, boasted of that and said in poetry: 'I preceded you to Islam entirely as a boy who had not reached the age of judgment.' And according to al-Shafi'i, his faith before puberty is not valid regarding worldly rulings, so he inherits from his disbelieving father and his polytheist wife does not separate from him because it is harmful. But it is valid regarding the rulings of the Hereafter because it is pure benefit in his regard. And we only said without obligation of performance because if the minor is asked to describe Islam and he does not describe it after that because he did not comprehend, his wife does not separate from him. And if performance were obligatory, his refusal would be disbelief. And if it is evil with no possibility of other than it, like disbelief, it is not considered pardoned. This is the second division. And by disbelief is meant apostasy, meaning if the minor apostatizes, his apostasy is considered according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding worldly and Hereafter rulings until his wife separates from him and he does not inherit from his Muslim relatives, but he is not killed because warfare from him before puberty was not found. And if anyone kills him, his blood is wasted and nothing is obligatory upon him like the adult apostate. And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, his apostasy is not valid regarding worldly rulings because it is pure harm. And we only ruled the validity of his faith because it is pure benefit. And what is revolving between the two matters, i.e., between being good at one time and evil at another, this is the third division, like prayer and the like. Performance from him is valid without liability and responsibility. If he begins it, he is not obligated to complete it and continue in it. And if he spoils it, making it up is not obligatory upon him. And in the validity of this performance without obligation upon him is pure benefit for him in that he becomes accustomed to performing them, so that does not become difficult after puberty.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the matn's classification of rulings (ahkam) for minors with partial legal capacity (ahliyyah qasirah). The author explains that the rulings in this chapter divide into six categories, but the focus here is on three of them that pertain to the rights of Allah (huquq Allah). Each category is defined by whether the act is purely good (hasan mahd), purely evil (qabih mahd), or mixed (da'ir bayna al-amrayn).

**First Division (Hasan Mahd):** Acts that are purely good with no possibility of evil, such as iman (faith). For such acts, the minor's performance is valid (sahih) but not obligatory (lazim). The proof is that Ali (RA) boasted of having accepted Islam as a child before puberty. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees regarding worldly rulings: the minor's faith is not valid for worldly purposes (e.g., inheritance, marital separation) because it would cause him harm (e.g., losing inheritance from a non-Muslim father). However, for the Hereafter, it is valid because it is pure benefit. The Hanafi position avoids obligation to prevent harm: if the minor were required to profess Islam and could not due to lack of understanding, he would be considered a disbeliever, leading to separation from his Muslim wife.

**Second Division (Qabih Mahd):** Acts that are purely evil, such as kufr (disbelief), specifically apostasy (riddah). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, a minor's apostasy is valid in both worldly and Hereafter rulings: his wife separates, he does not inherit from Muslims, but he is not killed because he cannot be considered a combatant before puberty. If someone kills him, no retaliation or blood money is due. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i rule that apostasy is not valid for worldly rulings because it is pure harm; only its validity for the Hereafter is accepted.

**Third Division (Da'ir):** Acts that can be good or evil depending on time, like salah. The minor's performance is valid without obligation. He is not required to start, complete, or make up if spoiled. The benefit is that the child becomes accustomed to worship, making it easier after puberty.

Throughout, the key principle is that rulings for minors are based on whether the act is purely beneficial (valid and effective), purely harmful (ineffective in worldly matters), or mixed (valid but not binding).

Key Terms
أهلية قاصرة (ahliyyah qasirah): Partial legal capacity; the capacity of a minor who can understand but is not fully responsible.
حسن لا يحتمل غيره (hasan la yahtamilu ghayruhu): An act that is intrinsically good with no possibility of being evil in any context, e.g., iman.
قبيح لا يحتمل غيره (qabih la yahtamilu ghayruhu): An act that is intrinsically evil with no possibility of being good, e.g., kufr/riddah.
لزوم (luzum): Obligation or binding effect; here, whether the act becomes binding upon the minor so that he is responsible for it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on a minor's apostasy (riddah) for worldly matters?
A) It is not valid because it is pure harm.
B) It is valid: the minor's wife separates, and he does not inherit from Muslim relatives, but he is not killed.
C) It is valid and the minor is killed as an adult apostate.
D) It is not valid for either worldly or Hereafter matters.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of a minor's act depends on whether it is purely beneficial, purely harmful, or mixed; only purely harmful acts are not pardoned, and mixed acts are valid without obligation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The Ruling on a Minor's Iman: Pure Good and Its Validity
Explains the Hanafi ruling on a minor's Iman as a pure good action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a minor's Iman, identify the proof from Ali's boast, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position regarding worldly rulings.

Core Idea
A minor's Iman is valid in both worlds because it is pure benefit—boasting of it proves its worth.

This passage from Ashraf al-Anwar addresses a foundational usul question: are the actions of a minor legally effective? The author classifies actions into six types and zeroes in on the category of 'pure good' (hasan mahd) exemplified by faith (Iman). We will unpack the Hanafi ruling, its proof, and the contrasting view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is Iman (faith), which falls under 'pure good' (hasan mahd) in the rights of Allah category. Pure good has no possibility of evil.
The author divides acts into six categories; this is the first under huquq Allah.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
A rational minor's Iman is valid (sahih) but not obligatory (lazim). This is called 'ada' qasirah (incomplete performance).
Obligation is suspended until puberty; validity means legal effects attach.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is the boast of Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) that he accepted Islam as a child. If childhood Iman were invalid, he would not have boasted.
This is a dalil naqli (transmitted proof) of type hadith mursal.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a minor's Iman is valid only for the afterlife, not for worldly rulings, due to potential harm (darar). The Hanafi response: Iman is pure benefit, so validity in both realms is appropriate.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) affects inheritance and marital rulings.

إلى سنة اقسام تو قسموں کی طرف ان اقسام کی طرف مصنف نے ترتیب وار اشارہ فرمایا ہے (تین میں حقوق اللہ سے تعلق رکھتی ہیں (۱) حسن (۲) صحیح اور وہ جو ان دونوں کے درمیان دائر ہے اسی طرح تین حقوق العباد سے متعلق میں نفع محض ضرر محض دائره بين النفع والضرر) پس منصف نے فرمایا : - فضل الله إن كَانَ حَسَناً لا يَحمِلُ غَيْرَهُ اور حق اللہ سے متعلق فعل (1) اگر وہ محض حسن ہو کہ کسی حال میں اس کے قبیح ہونے کا احتمال ہی نہ ہو مثلاً فعل ایمان تو صبی عاقل سے اسکی ادائے گی صحیح ہونے کا حکم لگا نا ضروری ہے البتہ ادا کرنا اس پر لازم نہیں ہے یہی پہلی قسم ہے یعنی اداء قاصرہ کی اہمیت کے حکم کی قسم اول ہے ہم نے اس کی اداء کے صحیح ہونے کا حکم اس لئے لگایا ہے کیونکہ حضرت علی رضی اللہ عنہ نے سی پر بطور فخر فرمایا تھا چنانچہ فرمایا تھا شعر اسلام قبول کرنے میں تمام لوگوں پر میں نے سبقت کی ۔ جب کہ میں بچہ ہی تھا ابھی تک حد بلوغ کو نہیں پہونچا تھا ( اگر بچپن کا ایمان لانا صحیح نہ ہوتا تو حضرت علی رضی اللہ عنہ اس پر فخر کیوں فرماتے ) امام شافعی کے نزدیک صہی کا ایمان لاجہ بالغ ہونے سے پہلے صحیح نہیں ہے احکام دینا کے حق میں لہذا اگر وہ ایمان لا چکا ہے تو باپ کے مرنے پر اس کا فر باپ کے مال کا بچہ دار ٹے ہو گا اور اس کی مشرکہ بیوی اس سے بائنہ نہ ہوگی کیونکہ ان احکام کے نفاذ میں اسکا نقصان (ضرر ) ہے اگر چہ احکام آخرت میں اس کا ایمان لانا صحیح ہو گا کیونکہ اس کے حق میں وہ نفع محض ہے وانما قلنا بلزوم اراء اور ہم نے کہا کہ ہوگا اس پر وائے ایمان واجب نہیں ہے تو اس کا فائدہ یہ ہو گا کہ اگر میں نا بالغ بچہ سے کلمہ اسلام معلوم کیا جائے اور وہ نہ جلا سکے تو اس کی مسلمان بیوی اس سے جدا نہ ہو گی اگر ادائے ایمان اس پر واجب ہوتی تو اس کا کلمہ اسلام نہ بتا سکتا کفر شمار کیا جا تا چنانچہ اس کے اور اس کی عورت کے درمیان ایسی صورت میں تفریق واقع ہو جائے گی۔
To six categories: the author has pointed in order to these categories. Three relate to the rights of Allah: (1) good, (2) correct, and what is between them. Similarly three relate to the rights of people: pure benefit, pure harm, and what is between benefit and harm. Then the author said: If it is good that does not admit anything else, and it is an act related to the rights of Allah, (1) if it is pure good with no possibility of being evil, like the act of faith, then from a rational minor its performance is ruled valid, but it is not obligatory upon him. This is the first type. We have ruled its performance valid because Ali (RA) boasted about it: 'I preceded all people in accepting Islam while I was a child, not yet having reached puberty.' If a child's faith were not valid, Ali (RA) would not have boasted. According to Shafi'i, a minor's faith before puberty is not valid for worldly rulings, so if he has brought faith, upon his father's death he does not inherit, and his polytheist wife does not become separated, because these rulings involve harm (ḍarar). However, in the rulings of the afterlife, his faith is valid because it is pure benefit for him. And we said that faith is not obligatory upon him. The benefit is that if a minor is asked to pronounce the Kalima and cannot, his Muslim wife is not separated from him; if pronouncing faith were obligatory, his inability to say it would be considered disbelief, causing separation.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal capacity of a rational minor (ṣabī ʿāqil) in matters of pure good (ḥasan maḥḍ) such as faith (īmān). The author classifies actions of a minor into six categories: three concerning rights of Allah (Ḥuqūq Allāh) and three concerning rights of people (Ḥuqūq al-ʿIbād). The first category under Ḥuqūq Allāh is pure good, exemplified by īmān. According to the Ḥanafī school, a minor's īmān is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) in both worldly and afterlife rulings, but it is not obligatory (lāzim) upon him. The proof is the statement of ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib (RA) who boasted of embracing Islam as a child. If a child's īmān were invalid, such boasting would be meaningless. The author contrasts the Ḥanafī position with that of Imām al-Shāfiʿī, who holds that a minor's īmān is valid only for the afterlife but not for worldly rulings, due to the harm (ḍarar) that would result (e.g., inheritance and marital separation). The Ḥanafīs argue that since īmān is pure benefit, it should be valid for both realms. Moreover, since īmān is not obligatory on a minor, if he cannot utter the Kalima, he is not considered a disbeliever, thus preserving his marriage. This ruling is from the established Ḥanafī doctrine (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah).

Key Terms
حسن محض (ḥasan maḥḍ): An act that is purely good with no possibility of being evil, like faith.
اداء قاصرہ (adā' qāṣirah): Incomplete performance: an act performed by a minor that is valid but not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥanafī school, is a rational minor's acceptance of Islam (īmān) valid for worldly rulings?
A) No, only for afterlife rulings
B) Yes, it is valid for both worldly and afterlife rulings
C) It depends on the guardian's permission
D) Only if the minor has reached the age of ten

Key Takeaway
A rational minor's Iman is valid in both realms because it is pure benefit, and the boast of Ali (RA) confirms its validity.

The Pure Evil Act: Minor's Apostasy and Its Legal Consequences
Ruling on minor's apostasy as pure evil act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a minor's commission of a pure evil act (qabīḥ maḥḍ) is legally effective, and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
A minor's apostasy is valid in Hanafi law because it nullifies a previously valid benefit.

This lesson explores a key distinction in Hanafi usul: acts that are purely evil (qabīḥ maḥḍ). While a minor's good acts are beneficial and valid, what about acts that are purely harmful? The case of a minor's apostasy reveals a fascinating dispute: does the child's faith make his subsequent apostasy effective? We will examine the reasoning of the Hanafi masters and the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category: pure evil (qabīḥ maḥḍ)
Distinguish between acts that are neutral or capable of good and evil, and those that are purely evil, like apostasy. This category triggers different rules for minors.
2
State the Hanafi position on a minor's apostasy
According to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad, the apostasy is valid and produces legal consequences: the minor's wife becomes separated, he inherits, but he is not executed.
The minor is not killed because he is not a combatant (muḥārib) before puberty.
3
Examine the opposing view
Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī argue that apostasy is pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ) and a minor's harmful acts are not valid to protect him from damage.
4
Trace the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rebuts by pointing to consistency: if a minor's faith (pure benefit) is valid, then its opposite (apostasy) must also be valid; the harm is outweighed by the need to maintain the symmetry of the ruling.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The concrete difference is on marriage and inheritance: Hanafi position leads to separation and inheritance; the opposing view leaves the marriage intact and no inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِن كَانَ قَبِيْحاً لا يَحْتَمِلُ غَيْرُهُ كَالكُفْرِ۔ اور اگر وہ عمل ایسا قبیح ہو کہ کسی حال میں اس کے حسن ہونے کا احتمال ہی نہ ہو جیسے کفر تو بچہ سے اس کا ارتکاب قابل معافی نہ ہو گا اہمیت اراء قاصرہ کی یہ دوسری قسم ہے اور یہاں پر کفر سے مراد اسلام قبول کرنے کے بعد مرتد ہو جاتا ہے یعنی اگر بچہ مرتد ہو جائے تو اس کے مرتد ہونے کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک دنیاو آخرت کے سلسلے میں یہاں تک کہ اس کی مسلمان بیوی اس سے یا سکہ ہو جائے گی اور یہ بچہ اپنے قرابت داروں کا وارث ہو گا البتہ بچہ مرتد ہونے کے بعد قتل نہ کیا جائے گا کیونکہ بالغ ہونے سے پہلے بچہ محاربین میں شمار نہیں ہو تا ہاں اگر کوئی مسلمان اس بچہ کو قتل کر دے تو اس کا خون بیکار را نگال چلا گیا اور اس پر دست و قصاص و غیر ہ کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔ جیسے مرند بالغ کے قتل کرنے سے کوئی چیز واجب نہیں ہوتی۔ وَعِندَ ابن يوسف والمنافعی اور امام ابو یوسف اور امام شافعی و نیادی احکام کے سلسلے میں نا بالغ بچے کا مرتد ہونا صحیح نہ مانا جائے گا کیونکہ یہ ضرور محض ہے نا بالغ کے ایمان کے صحیح ہونے کا حکم ہم نے اس لئے لگایا ہے کیونکہ وہ نفع محض ہے۔
And if it is evil that does not admit anything else, like disbelief: if the act is such evil that there is no possibility of it being good, like disbelief, then a child's commission of it is not excusable. This is the second type. Disbelief here means apostasy after accepting Islam. So if a child apostatizes, his apostasy is considered; according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, in both worldly and afterlife matters, even his Muslim wife becomes separated from him, and he inherits from his relatives. However, the child is not killed for apostasy because before puberty he is not counted among combatants. But if a Muslim kills such a child, his blood is wasted, and no diya or qisas is obligatory, just as killing an adult apostate incurs nothing. According to Ibn Yusuf (Abu Yusuf) and Shafi'i, in worldly rulings a minor's apostasy is not considered valid, because it is pure harm; we ruled the minor's faith valid only because it is pure benefit.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage discusses the second category of a minor's actions: pure evil (qabīḥ maḥḍ), exemplified by apostasy (riddah) after having accepted Islam. According to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī, a minor's apostasy is valid and produces legal consequences in both worldly and afterlife matters: his Muslim wife becomes separated (bāʾin), and he inherits from his relatives. However, he is not executed because he is not a combatant (muḥārib) before puberty. If someone kills him, there is no blood money or retaliation, similar to an adult apostate. In contrast, Abū Yūsuf and Imām al-Shāfiʿī hold that a minor's apostasy is not valid for worldly rulings because it is pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ), analogously to the ruling of faith which they restrict to benefits. The Ḥanafī position distinguishes by considering that apostasy involves harm, but since the minor has already acquired faith (a benefit), the subsequent apostasy is treated as a positive evil act that the minor can commit with effect. This ruling is from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Key Terms
قبيح محض (qabīḥ maḥḍ): An act that is purely evil with no possibility of being good, like apostasy.
مرتد (murtadd): One who abandons Islam after having accepted it; a minor can be a murtadd in Ḥanafī law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad, what happens to a minor's marriage if he apostatizes?
A) The marriage remains valid
B) The wife is separated (bāʾin)
C) Marriage is suspended until puberty
D) The minor is divorced automatically

Key Takeaway
A minor's commission of a pure evil act like apostasy is legally effective in Hanafī law, separating marriage and permitting inheritance, but without capital punishment.

Category Three: Between Good and Evil (Ibadat) of a Minor
Ruling on minor's performance of bodily acts of worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the category of dāʾir bayn al-amrayn and apply the Hanafi ruling that a rational minor's bodily worship is valid but not obligatory, explaining the rationale of istiḥsān.

Core Idea
A minor's worship is valid training, not a binding obligation.

This lesson introduces the third category of acts according to the Ḥanafī school: actions that are between good and evil, specifically bodily acts of worship (ʿibādāt badaniyyah) performed by a rational minor. You will learn why such acts are considered valid (ṣaḥīḥ) but not obligatory (lāzim), and how this ruling serves the minor's long-term religious development. The source passage from Ashraf al-Anwār explains that the minor's performance is an incomplete performance (adāʾ qāṣirah) that accustoms him to worship after puberty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Category
The passage refers to 'dāʾir bayn al-amrayn'—acts that are considered good in some contexts and evil in others. For a minor, bodily acts of worship (prayer, fasting) fall into this category because they can be meritorious if done correctly but non-obligatory due to the minor's lack of full legal capacity (taklīf).
This distinguishes it from acts that are inherently good (maḥmūd) or inherently evil (madhmūm).
2
State the Ruling
The Ḥanafī ruling: if a rational minor (ṣabbī ʿāqil) begins an act of bodily worship, it is valid (ṣaḥīḥ). However, he is not obligated to complete it, and if he breaks it, no make-up (qaḍā) is required. The act is not binding (lāzim).
This is called 'adāʾ qāṣirah' (incomplete performance): the act achieves its essential effect but does not impose a continuous obligation.
3
Understand the Rationale
The wisdom (ḥikmah) is to train the minor in worship. By performing these acts, the minor becomes accustomed to them, so after puberty (bulūgh) they are not burdensome. The ruling is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference) considering the benefit to the minor.
The dalīl is not a specific text but the principle of ease and training.
4
Contrast with Adult Rulings
For a matured person, starting a prayer makes it obligatory to complete; breaking it requires qaḍā. For a minor, there is no such obligation. This contrast highlights the special legal status of the minor in the Ḥanafī school.
The difference stems from the minor's incomplete legal capacity (ahliyyat al-adāʾ nāqiṣah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا هُوَ دَائِر بَيْنَ الأمرين (۳) یادہ جو دائر ہو دونوں کے درمیان یعنی کبھی وہ حسن شمار کیا جائے تو دوسرے وقت میں تیج شمار ہو اور یہ ادائے قاصرہ کے حکم کی تیسری قسم کا جان ہے جیسے نماز اور اس قسم کی دوسری بدنی عباد نہیں تو اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ مہمی عاقل سے اس کی ادائے بھی صحیح ہو گی البتہ اس کے ذمہ اس کا پورا کرنا یا اس کا تدارک واجب نہ ہو گا لہذا اگر اس نے کسی فصل کو شروع کر دیا تو اس کا پورا کرنا واجب نہیں ہے نہ اسکو یہ قرار رکھنا ضروری ہے اور اگر اس کو توڑ دے (فاسد کر دے) تو اس پر قضاء اجب نہ ہو گی اس کی ادے گی کے صحیح ہونے اور صہی پر اداء کے لازم ہونے میں اس کا نفع محض ہے اس وجہ سے کہ ایسا کرنے سے وہ فعل کے اداء کرنے کا عادی ہو جائے گا لہذا ادائے گی بعد البلوغ اس پر دشوار نہ ہوگی۔
And what is between the two matters (3): that which is between them, meaning sometimes it is considered good and at other times evil, and this is the third type of the ruling of incomplete performance, like prayer and other bodily acts of worship. Its ruling: a rational minor's performance is valid, but he is not obligated to complete it or make up for it. Therefore, if he begins an act, completing it is not obligatory, nor is it necessary to maintain it, and if he breaks it (invalidates it), no make-up (qaḍā) is required upon him. The benefit of ruling his performance valid and not obligatory is that by doing so he becomes accustomed to performing the act, so after puberty it will not be difficult for him.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage covers the third category: actions that are between good and evil, such as bodily acts of worship (e.g., ṣalāh, ṣawm). According to the Ḥanafī school, a rational minor's performance of such acts is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) but not obligatory (lāzim). The minor may start, but he is not required to complete it; if he breaks it, no qaḍā is due. The wisdom (ḥikmah) is to train the child in worship so that after puberty it becomes easy. This ruling is based on istiḥsān and consideration of benefit (nafʿ maḥḍ for the minor). The authority weight is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Key Terms
دائر بين الأمرين (dāʾir bayn al-amrayn): An act that can be good or evil depending on circumstances, like bodily worship for a minor.
اداء قاصرہ (adāʾ qāṣirah): Incomplete performance: act is valid but not binding.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows departing from strict analogy for a stronger reason, like training a minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a rational minor begins praying voluntarily and then deliberately breaks the prayer, what is the Ḥanafī ruling?
A) He must make it up (qaḍā) after puberty
B) No make-up is required
C) He must continue unless excused
D) His prayer is invalid from the start

Key Takeaway
A rational minor's bodily worship is valid training without obligation, allowing gradual religious habituation.

The Fourth Category: Pure Benefit in Huquq al-Ibad
Ruling on minor's acceptance of pure benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a minor's acceptance of a pure benefit transaction (e.g., gift) is valid without guardian consent, and apply it to identify such acts.

Core Idea
Pure benefit transactions of a minor are valid without guardian consent because there is no harm.

In this Usul al-Fiqh lesson, we examine the fourth category of actions concerning the rights of people: pure benefit (nafʿ maḥḍ), such as accepting a gift or charity. The Hanafi school recognizes that a rational minor may directly perform such acts without the guardian's permission. Understanding this exception is essential for correctly applying the legal capacity rules in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two broad categories
Begin by recalling that actions are divided into rights of Allah (ḥuqūq Allāh) and rights of people (ḥuqūq al-ʿibād). This passage deals with the latter.
Rights of Allah include acts like prayer and fasting.
2
Subdivide rights of people
Within rights of people, the scholar further divides into three: pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed. Locate the phrase 'nafʿ maḥḍ' (pure benefit) in the passage.
Pure benefit is exemplified by accepting a gift or charity.
3
Identify the rule for pure benefit
The passage states that the minor's direct action (mubāsharah) is valid without the guardian's consent or permission. Parse that ruling.
The Arabic: فَصَحُ مُبَاشَرَتُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ رَضَاءِ الْوَلِيِّ وَإِذْنِهِ
4
Understand the rationale
The underlying reason is that pure benefit involves no harm to the minor; therefore, there is no need for guardian protection. This principle (illah) is derived from the absence of harm.
This is a key usul point: the absence of harm triggers the rule.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider a minor receiving a monetary gift. According to this rule, ownership transfers immediately upon acceptance, without guardian involvement.
Contrast with a sale, which is not pure benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا كَانَ مِنْ غَيْرِ حُقوق الله تَعَالَى إِنْ كَانَ نَفْعًا مَحْضَا كَقَبُول الهبة والصدقة فَصَحُ مُبَاشَرَتُهُ أَيْ مُبَاشَرَةُ الصَّبَي مِنْ غَيْرِ رَضَاءِ الْوَلِيُّ وَإِذْنِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ الْقِسْمُ الرابع
And what is from other than the rights of Allah Almighty, if it is pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity, then his direct performance is valid, that is, the minor's direct action without the guardian's consent or permission, and this is the fourth category.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth category concerning rights of people (Ḥuqūq al-ʿIbād): transactions of pure benefit (nafʿ maḥḍ) such as accepting a gift (hibah) or charity (ṣadaqah). According to the Ḥanafī school, a rational minor may directly perform such acts without the guardian's approval (walī). The underlying reason is that these acts involve only benefit (gain of property without loss), so there is no harm to the minor. The validity of the minor's acceptance is effective, and ownership is established. This ruling is from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Key Terms
نفع محض (nafʿ maḥḍ): Pure benefit with no element of harm, like receiving a gift.
حقوق العباد (ḥuqūq al-ʿibād): Rights of people; transactions and interactions between individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ḥanafī fiqh, can a rational minor accept a gift without his guardian's permission?
A) No, it is invalid
B) Yes, it is valid
C) Only if the gift is from a relative
D) Only if the guardian later approves

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a minor's acceptance of a pure benefit transaction like a gift is valid without guardian consent.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Pure Harm Transactions of a Minor: General Rule and Need-Based Exception
Ruling on minor's acts of pure harm like divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rule on a minor's pure harm acts, apply the exception based on istiḥsān for necessity, and evaluate the intra-madhhab disagreement on the effect of a minor's divorce.

Core Idea
A minor's pure harm act is generally void, but divorce becomes effective when necessity demands, per istiḥsān.

In Hanafi usul, acts of a minor that are purely harmful (ḍarar maḥḍ) — such as divorce, will, or donation — are normally void because they entail loss without benefit. However, this principle is not absolute. Senior jurist Shams al-A'imma invoked istiḥsān to allow a minor's divorce in cases of compelling need, such as the wife's conversion to Islam or the minor's apostasy. This lesson unpacks the rule, the exception, and the scholarly discussion among the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Locate the statement that pure harm acts of a minor are void in origin (yaṣḥub aṣalan). Understand that this is the default position based on the principle that a minor cannot incur loss without worldly benefit.
The text mentions divorce, will, manumission, charity, gift, and loan as examples.
2
Recognize the exception and its basis
Find where Shams al-A'imma states that a minor's divorce is effective 'when need calls for it' (idhā daʿat ilayhi ḥājah). Note that this exception is founded on istiḥsān (juristic preference) over the general rule.
The examples show that necessity overrides the voidness.
3
Analyze the examples of necessity
Examine each scenario: (1) wife's conversion to Islam, (2) minor's apostasy, (3) minor's impotence. For each, note which Hanafi imam considers the resulting separation a valid divorce.
Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad accept the divorce in case 1; Muḥammad alone in case 2; some scholars in case 3.
4
Map the intra-madhhab dispute
Identify the dissenting view of Abū Yūsuf, who holds that a minor's refusal to convert does not effect a divorce. Contrast with the majority position. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): whether separation occurs or not.
The text tilts toward the majority view (Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad).
5
Formulate the usul principle
Derive the operative rule: 'Acts of pure harm are void for a minor, except when necessity (ḥājah) requires otherwise, in which case they are effective based on istiḥsān.'
This is a clear example of a general rule being restricted by an exceptional cause (takhṣīṣ bi al-istikāra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الضَّرَرِ الْمَحْضِ الَّذِي لَا يَشْوْبُهُ نَفْعُ دُنْيَاوِي كَالطُّلاقِ وَالْوَصِيَّةِ ونحوهما من العتاق والتصدقِ وَالْبَةِ وَالْقَرْض يَبْطُلُ أَصَلا فَإِنَّ فِيهَا إِزَالَةً ملك مِنْ غَيْرِ نَفْعٍ يَعُودُ إِلَيْهِ وَلَكِنْ قَالَ شَمْسُ الأَئِمَّةِ إِنْ طَلاق الصبي واقع إِذَا دَعَتْ إِلَيْهِ حَاجَةٌ الأَتَرى أَنْهُ إذَا أَسْلَمَتْ امْرَأَتُهُ يُعْرَضَ عَلَيْهِ الإِسْلاَمُ فَإِنْ أَبَى فُرْق بَيْنَهُمَا وَهُوَ طَلاق عِنْدَ أَبي حَنِيفَةٌ وَمَحْمُدٌ وَإِذَا ارْتُدْ وقَعَتِ الْفُرْقَةُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْن إمْرَأتِهِ وَهُوَ طَلاق عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ وَإِذَا كَانَ مَجْبُوْباً فَخَاصَمَتُهُ امْرَأَتُهُ وَطَلَبَت التَّفْرِيق كَانَ ذالك طلاقًا عِندَ الْبَعْض فَعْلِمَ أنْ حُكْمَ الطلاق ثابت فِي حَقَّهِ عِنْدَ الحاجة وهذ هُوَ القِسمُ الخَامِسُ مِنْهُ
And on pure harm that is not mixed with any worldly benefit, such as divorce, will, and similar acts from freeing slaves, charity, gift, and loan, it is void in origin, because they involve removal of ownership without any benefit returning to him. However, Shams al-A'imma said that a minor's divorce occurs when need calls for it. Do you not see that if his wife accepts Islam, he is presented with Islam; if he refuses, separation is made between them, and it is divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And if he apostatizes, separation occurs between him and his wife, and it is divorce according to Muhammad. And if he is impotent and his wife sues him and demands separation, that is divorce according to some. So it is known that the ruling of divorce is established in his case when need arises. This is the fifth category.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage covers the fifth category: acts of pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ) in rights of people, such as divorce (ṭalāq), will (waṣiyyah), manumission (ʿitq), charity (ṣadaqah), gift (hibah), and loan (qarḍ). In principle, such acts are void (bāṭil) for a minor because they involve removal of ownership without any worldly benefit returning to him. However, Shams al-A'imma (a senior Ḥanafī scholar) qualifies that a minor's divorce is effective when necessity (ḥājah) demands. Examples: if the minor's wife converts to Islam and he refuses to convert, the judge separates them, and this separation is considered a divorce according to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad. If the minor apostatizes, separation occurs and is considered divorce according to Muḥammad. If the minor is impotent (majbūb) and his wife seeks annulment, some scholars consider it a divorce. Thus, the ruling of divorce is established for the minor when there is a need, even though pure harm transactions are generally void. This exception is based on istiḥsān. The authority weight is from the later Ḥanafī works (al-Mutākhkhirīn).

Key Terms
ضرر محض (ḍarar maḥḍ): Pure harm with no worldly benefit, such as a minor divorcing or donating property.
شمس الأئمة (Shams al-A'imma): Title of the Ḥanafī jurist al-Sarakhsī (or possibly another), known for his work al-Mabsūṭ.
حاجة (ḥājah): Necessity that permits an exception to the general rule to prevent hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shams al-A'imma, when is a minor's divorce considered effective?
A) Never, it is always void
B) Only when the minor is ten years old
C) When necessity arises, e.g., wife's conversion to Islam and minor's refusal
D) Only if the guardian agrees

Key Takeaway
A minor's pure harm acts are void in origin, but divorce becomes effective when necessity demands, demonstrating the Hanafi use of istiḥsān to override general rules.
